But if someone sins against God, it is not within our power to judge. For the divine Scripture says: If a man sins against another man, the priest will pray for him. But if someone sins against God, who will pray for him? On the contrary, we are kind in the face of God's injustice and exercise hatred in our insults. However, if a brother needs correction, let him be corrected privately, so that if he has lost his sense of shame and honor, he may not remain in sin. And if indeed he listens, we have gained his soul, and through the salvation of another, our own salvation is also obtained. But if he refuses to listen, let a brother be brought in. And if he does not listen to him, let a third be brought in, either out of a desire to correct or to meet under witnesses. But if he does not want to listen to them either, then he should be spoken to by many, so that they may hold him in contempt, and let him be saved from shame who could not be saved by modesty. However, when it is said, 'Let him be to you as a heathen and a tax collector,' it is shown that he is held in even greater contempt, who carries out the works of unbelievers under the name of the faithful, than those who are openly Gentiles. For publicans, according to metaphor, are those who pursue worldly gains and collect taxes through business dealings and fraud, as well as theft and wicked perjuries.

<h2 id='tocuniq384'>(Vers. 18.)</h2>

(Verse 18.) Truly I tell you: whatever you bind on earth will be bound in heaven, and whatever you loose on earth will be loosed in heaven. For he had said: But if he does not listen to the Church, let him be to you as a Gentile and a tax collector, and this hidden reply could be the contemptuous brother's response, or a tacit thought, if you despise me, I despise you, if you condemn me, you will be condemned by my judgment: he gave the apostles the power to know that those who are condemned by such will have their human judgment confirmed by divine judgment, and whatever is bound on earth will be bound in heaven as well.


<h2 id='tocuniq385'>(Vers. 19, 20.)</h2>

(Verse 19, 20.) Again I say to you: if two of you agree on earth about anything they ask, it will be done for them by my Father in heaven. For where two or three are gathered in my name, there am I among them. The above saying has prompted us towards unity. Therefore, a reward is promised, so that we may strive eagerly for peace, since it is said that He will be present among two or three. According to the example of a tyrant, who wanted to prove his two captured friends - when one returned to see his mother and gave his friend as a surety for his return - by holding one, he would release the other. And when he returned on the appointed day, marveling at the faith of both, he asked that they have a third. We can also understand this spiritually, that where the spirit and soul and body agree, and they have not war among themselves of diverse wills (or desires); with the flesh lusting against the spirit, and the spirit against the flesh, they shall obtain from the Father whatever they ask for. And there is no doubt that the request for good things is when the body wants what the spirit wants.


<h2 id='tocuniq386'>(Vers. 21, 22.)</h2>

(Verse 21, 22.) Then Peter came to him and said: Lord, how often shall my brother sin against me, and I forgive him? Up to seven times? Jesus said to him: I do not say to you, up to seven times, but up to seventy times seven. The saying of the Lord stuck in his mind, and it cannot be broken like a triple cord. Above, he had said: See that you do not despise one of these little ones; and he added: If your brother sins against you, go and rebuke him between you and him alone; and he had promised a reward, saying: If two of you agree on earth about anything they ask, it will be done for them by my Father in heaven; and I will be among them. The Apostle Peter, being provoked, asks how many times he should forgive his brother who sins against him, and when he suggests seven times, Jesus responds: Not seven times, but seventy times seven, meaning four hundred and ninety times, so that he may forgive his sinning brother in a day as many times as he is unable to sin.

<h2 id='tocuniq387'>(Vers. 23.)</h2>

(Verse 23.) Therefore, the kingdom of heaven is likened to a human king who wanted to settle accounts with his servants. It is common for the Syrians and especially the Palestinians to join parables to every speech: so that what cannot be understood by the listeners through a simple instruction, may be understood through a comparison and examples. So he commanded Peter, using the comparison of a king and master, and servants, who owed ten thousand 143 talents to the master, to ask for forgiveness, and he obtained it; so that he himself would also forgive his fellow servants who had committed lesser sins. That king and lord forgave the servant debtor ten thousand talents so easily, how much more should servants forgive their fellow servants lesser offenses? In order to make it clearer, let us say under this example: If one of us commits adultery, murder, sacrilege, greater crimes of ten thousand talents, they are forgiven when they ask for forgiveness, if they themselves forgive lesser sins. But if, because of an insult, we are unforgiving and, because of a bitter word, we perpetuate discord: do we not seem rightly to be imprisoned? And in the example of our work, should we not act in a way that the pardon for our greater sins is not relaxed?


<h2 id='tocuniq388'>(Vers. 24 seqq.)</h2>

(Verse 24 onwards) And when he began to settle accounts, one was brought to him who owed him ten thousand talents. But since he could not pay, his master ordered him to be sold, along with his wife and children and all that he had, and payment to be made. So the servant fell on his knees, imploring him, 'Have patience with me, and I will pay you everything.' And out of pity for him, the master of that servant released him and forgave him the debt. But when that same servant went out, he found one of his fellow servants who owed him a hundred denarii, and seizing him, he began to choke him, saying, 'Pay what you owe.'" } And falling down, his fellow servant begged him, saying: Have patience with me, and I will repay you all. But he would not: but went and threw him into prison, till he paid the debt. Now his fellow servants seeing what was done, were very much grieved, and they came and told their lord all that was done. Then his lord called him: and said to him: You wicked servant, I forgave you all the debt, because you besought me. Did it not behoove you also to have mercy on your fellow servant, even as I had mercy on you? And his lord being angry, delivered him to the torturers until he should pay all the debt. I know certain ones who owe ten thousand talents, to interpret the devil, whose wife and children they wish to sell, who persist in wickedness, foolishness, and evil thoughts. For just as the wife of the just is called wisdom, so the wife of the unjust and sinner is called folly. But how can the master forgive him ten thousand talents, when he did not forgive us, his fellow servants, a hundred denarii? And it is not in accordance with Ecclesiastical interpretation, nor is it acceptable to wise men.

<h2 id='tocuniq389'>(Vers. 35.)</h2>

(Verse 35) And thus my heavenly Father will do to you, if each one of you does not forgive his brother from your hearts. This is a fearsome sentence, if the sentence of God is turned and changed according to our understanding. If we do not forgive our brothers for small things, great things will not be forgiven to us by God. And because each one can say: I have nothing against him, he himself knows: he has God as judge, I do not care what he wants to do, I have forgiven him; he confirms his sentence, and overturns all pretense of fake peace, saying: If each one of you does not forgive his brother from your hearts.


<h2 id='tocuniq390'>(Chap. XIX.—Vers. 1 seqq.)</h2>

(Chapter 19, Verses 1 and following) And it came to pass, when Jesus had finished these sayings, he departed from Galilee and came into the borders of Judea beyond the Jordan; and great multitudes followed him, and he healed them there. And the Pharisees came unto him, tempting him, and saying, Is it lawful for a man to put away his wife for every cause? He had come from Galilee to Judea, and therefore the faction of the Pharisees and Scribes questioned him, whether it is lawful for a man to put away his wife for any cause, so that they might catch him in a trap with their reasoning, and whatever answer he gives, they can use it against him. If someone were to say that wives should be divorced for any reason and others should be married, he will be seen as preaching against chastity. But if he were to respond that not every cause should lead to divorce, he will be seen as guilty of sacrilege; and he will be judged as going against the teaching of Moses and, through Moses, of God. Therefore, the Lord tempers his response so that their argument is surpassed, bringing forth Holy Scripture as testimony and opposing the natural law, and putting the first sentence of God against the second sentence; which was granted not by the will of God, but by the necessity of sinners.


<h2 id='tocuniq391'>(Vers. 4.)</h2>

(Verse 4.) And he answered and said to them: Have you not read that he who created them from the beginning made them male and female? This is written in the beginning of Genesis. But in saying male and female, he indicates the necessity of second marriages. For he does not say males and females, which was the subject of the previous divorce question, but male and female, so that the union of one spouse might be established.

<h2 id='tocuniq392'>(Vers. 5, 6.)</h2>

(Vers. 5, 6.) And he said: for this reason a man will leave his father and mother, and be joined to his wife: and they will become one flesh. So now they are no longer two, but one flesh. Likewise, he said, they will be joined to their wife, not wives. And they will become one flesh. The reward of marriage is for two to become one flesh. Chastity united with the spirit, the spirit becomes one.

What therefore God has joined together, let no man separate. God has joined them, by making the flesh of man and woman one: this man cannot separate, unless perchance God alone. Man separates, when on account of the desire for a second wife, he dismisses the first. God separates, who also joined together, when by consent on account of the service of God (because the time is narrow) we have wives, as if not having (I Cor. VII).

<h2 id='tocuniq393'>(Vers. 7.)</h2>

(V. 7) They said to him: So why did Moses command to give a writ of divorce and to dismiss? They laid open the pretext which they had prepared. And certainly the Lord had not put forth His own opinion, but had remembered the history of old and the commandments of God.

<h2 id='tocuniq394'>(Vers. 8.)</h2>

(Verse 8.) He said to them: because Moses allowed you to divorce your wives due to the hardness of your hearts; but from the beginning it was not so. What he is saying is this: Can God be opposed to himself, so that he would command one thing and then change his decision with a new order? It is not to be understood in this way: but when Moses saw that because of the desire for second wives, who were either wealthier, younger, or more attractive, the first wives were being killed or living a bad life, he chose to tolerate discord rather than perpetuate hatred and murder (Deut. XXIV). At the same time, consider that God did not allow it to you because of the hardness of your heart, but Moses, so that, according to the Apostle Paul's advice (I Cor. VII), it might be the counsel of man, not the commandment of God.

<h2 id='tocuniq395'>(Vers. 9.)</h2>

(Verse 9) But I say to you that whoever divorces his wife, except for sexual immorality, and marries another, commits adultery. And whoever marries her who is divorced commits adultery. Only sexual immorality is what overcomes the affection of a wife: indeed, when she has divided one flesh into another, and has separated herself from her husband by fornication, she ought not to be held bound; lest she also make the man under a curse, with the Scriptures saying: Whoever holds on to an adulteress, he is foolish and wicked. Therefore, wherever there is fornication, or even suspicion of fornication, a wife is freely divorced. And because it could happen that someone accuses an innocent person and falsely attributes an old crime to the second marriage, the first wife is commanded to be divorced so that, while the first wife is still alive, the husband does not have a second wife. For what He says is as follows: If you divorce your wife not because of lust but because of injury, why do you expose yourself to the danger of unhappy previous marriages and enter into new ones? Moreover, because it was possible for the wife to also divorce her husband according to the same law, the same caution is prescribed so that she does not take another husband. And because a prostitute, and one who had once been an adulteress, did not fear disgrace, a similar instruction is given to the husband, that if he marries such a woman, he is to be held guilty of adultery.

<h2 id='tocuniq396'>(Vers. 10.)</h2>

(Verse 10.) His disciples said to him: If such is the case with a man and his wife, it is not expedient to marry. It is a heavy burden for wives, for apart from the cause of fornication, they cannot be divorced. For what if she is drunken, if she is wrathful, if she has bad habits, if she is luxurious, if she is gluttonous, if she is fickle, if she is quarrelsome, if she is abusive, she must be tolerated. We want it, we don't want it, she must be endured. For when we were free, we voluntarily subjected ourselves to servitude. Therefore, seeing the heavy burden of wives, the apostles express the sentiment of their minds and say: if this is the cause, it is not expedient for a man to marry.

<h2 id='tocuniq397'>(Vers. 11.)</h2>

(Verse 11) He said to them: Not all can accept this word, but only those to whom it is given. Let no one think that under this word, or fate, or fortune is introduced: these are the virgins to whom it has been given by God, or whom some chance has led to this, but it is given to those who have asked, who have desired, who have worked to receive it. For to everyone who asks, it will be given, and the one who seeks will find, and to the one who knocks, it will be opened (Matthew 7:8 and Luke 11:10)." }


<h2 id='tocuniq398'>(Vers. 12.)</h2>

(Verse 12.) For there are eunuchs who were born so from their mother's womb; and there are eunuchs who were made so by men; and there are eunuchs who made themselves eunuchs for the sake of the kingdom of heaven. Let him who is able to receive it, receive it. There are three kinds of eunuchs: those who are born of the flesh of their mother, and those who are made by human agency, and those who castrated themselves for the sake of the kingdom of heaven; and those who, though they are capable of being men, become eunuchs for the sake of Christ." } These are promised a reward: however, to those previous ones for whom the necessity of chastity is not a matter of will, nothing at all is owed. We can also say differently. There are eunuchs who are born from their mother's womb, who are of a colder nature and have no desire for pleasure. And there are others who are made by men: whom either philosophers create, or for the sake of idol worship are softened into women: either they simulate chastity due to heretical persuasion, in order to lie about the truth of religion. But none of them attain the kingdom of heaven, except those who have castrated themselves for the sake of Christ. Hence he infers: He who can receive it, let him receive it; in order that each one may consider his own strength, whether he can fulfill the precepts of virginity and chastity. For chastity is alluring in itself, attracting everyone to it. But one's strength must be considered, so that he who can receive it, may receive it. It is as if the voice of the Lord were exhorting, and inciting his soldiers to the reward of chastity. Whoever can understand, let them understand; whoever can fight, let them fight, conquer and triumph.

<h2 id='tocuniq399'>(Vers. 13, 14.)</h2>

(Vers. 13, 14.) Then little children were brought to him, that he might lay his hands on them and pray. But the disciples rebuked them. But Jesus said to them, 'Let the little children come to me, and do not hinder them, for to such belongs the kingdom of heaven.'

<h2 id='tocuniq400'>(Vers. 15.)</h2>

(Verse 15.) Let the little children come to me, and do not hinder them, for to such belongs the kingdom of heaven. And when he had laid his hands on them, he went away from there. He said significantly, to such, not to those: to show that it is not age that rules but character; and to those who have similar innocence and simplicity, a reward is promised. The apostle also agrees with this sentiment: Brothers, do not be children in your thinking, but be infants in evil, and mature in your thinking (I Cor. XIV, 20).


<h2 id='tocuniq401'>(Vers. 16.)</h2>

(Verse 16) And behold, one came and said to him: Good Teacher, what good thing shall I do that I may have eternal life? He said to him. This man who asks how to obtain eternal life is young, wealthy, and proud; and according to another evangelist, he asks not out of a desire to learn, but out of a desire to test (Mark 10).

<h2 id='tocuniq402'>(Vers. 17.)</h2>

(Verse 17.) Why are you asking me about goodness? There is only one good God. Because he had called the teacher good and had not confessed him to be God, or the Son of God, he learns that even though a holy man is not good in comparison to God, of whom it is said: Give thanks to the Lord, for he is good (Ps. CXVII, 1). But lest anyone think that the goodness of the Son of God is excluded in that he is called a good God, we read in another place: The good shepherd lays down his life for his sheep (John X). And in the Prophet, a good spirit and a good land. Therefore, the Savior did not reject the testimony of goodness. But he excluded the error of teachers without God.

<h2 id='tocuniq403'>(Vers. 18, 19.)</h2>

(Verse 18, 19.) But if you want to enter into life, keep the commandments. He said to Him: Which ones? Jesus said: You shall not murder. You shall not commit adultery. You shall not steal. You shall not bear false witness. Honor your father and mother: and you shall love your neighbor as yourself. This young man is a tempter, and from this we can prove that when the Lord said to him, If you want to enter into life, keep the commandments, he deceitfully asks what those commandments are: as if he himself has not read them, or as if the Lord could command things contrary to God.


<h2 id='tocuniq404'>(Vers. 20.)</h2>

(Verse 20.) The young man said to him: All these things I have kept from my youth, what do I still lack? Jesus said to him: The young man is lying. For if he had fulfilled the commandment that is written: You shall love your neighbor as yourself, how then, upon hearing, Go, sell what you have, and give to the poor, did he depart sorrowful, because he had many possessions?

<h2 id='tocuniq405'>(Vers. 21.)</h2>

(Verse 21) If you want to be perfect, go, sell what you have and give to the poor, and you will have treasure in heaven; and come, follow me. It is in our power to choose whether we want to be perfect. However, whoever wants to be perfect must sell what they have, and not sell just a part, as Ananias and Sapphira did (Acts 5); but sell everything and give it all to the poor, and thus prepare for themselves treasure in the kingdom of heaven. 149 And this is not enough for perfection, unless after despising riches he follows the Savior, that is, having left behind evil, he does good. For it is easier to despise a bag than pleasure. Many, leaving behind riches, do not follow the Lord. But he who imitates Him follows the Lord and walks in His footsteps. For whoever says that he believes in Christ should walk just as He walked (1 John 2:6).


<h2 id='tocuniq406'>(Vers. 22.)</h2>

(Verse 22.) He went away sad, for he had many possessions. This is the sadness that leads to death. And the reason for the sadness is given, that he had many possessions, that is, thorns and thistles, which choked the Lord's seed.

<h2 id='tocuniq407'>(Vers. 23.)</h2>

(Verse 23) Jesus said to his disciples: Truly I say to you, it is difficult for a rich person to enter the kingdom of heaven. And how did Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob, who were rich, enter the kingdom of heaven (Genesis 13 and 36), and in the Gospel of Matthew and Zacchaeus, they are proclaimed by the testimony of the Lord, leaving their riches behind? But it should be considered that at the time they entered, they ceased to be rich. Therefore, they will not enter as long as they are rich. And yet because riches are difficult to despise, he did not say: It is impossible for the rich to enter the kingdom of heaven, but difficult. Where difficult is placed, not impossibility is suggested; but rarity is demonstrated.

<h2 id='tocuniq408'>(Vers. 24 seqq.)</h2>

(Vers. 24 seqq.) And again I say to you: It is easier for a camel to go through the eye of a needle than for a rich man to enter the kingdom of heaven. When His disciples heard this, they were greatly astonished, saying: Who then can be saved? But Jesus looked at them and said: With men this is impossible, but with God all things are possible. This statement shows that it is not difficult, but impossible. For just as a camel cannot enter through the eye of a needle, so a rich man cannot enter the kingdom of heaven; no rich man will be saved. But if we read Isaiah, how the camels of Midian and Ephah come to Jerusalem with gifts and offerings (Isa. 60): and those who were previously bent and twisted by the corruption of vices enter the gates of Jerusalem, we will see how even these camels, to whom the rich are compared, can enter through the narrow gate and the narrow path that leads to life (Above, 7). But when the disciples questioned and marveled at the severity of his words, He tempered the severity of his sentence with his mercy, saying: What is impossible for men is possible for God.

<h2 id='tocuniq409'>(Vers. 27.)</h2>

(Verse 27.) Then Peter answered and said to Him, 'See, we have left everything and followed You. What then will we have?' Great confidence: Peter was a fisherman, not wealthy, he sought food by hand and skill, and yet he speaks confidently, 'We have left everything.' And because it is not enough to just leave, he adds what is perfect: 'and followed You.' We have done what You commanded, so what reward will You give us?

<h2 id='tocuniq410'>(Vers. 28.)</h2>

(Verse 28) But Jesus said to them: Amen I say to you, that you who have followed me, in the regeneration, when the Son of man shall sit on the seat of his majesty, you also shall sit on twelve seats, judging the twelve tribes of Israel. He did not say: those who have left everything, for even Crates the philosopher did this, and many others despised riches; but those who have followed me: this belongs specifically to the apostles and believers. In regeneration, when the Son of man shall sit on the throne of his majesty, you also shall sit on twelve thrones judging the twelve tribes of Israel: because they believed not when you believed.

<h2 id='tocuniq411'>(Vers. 29, 30.)</h2>

(Verse 29, 30.) And everyone who has left houses, or brothers, or sisters, or father, or mother, or wife, or children, or lands for my name's sake, shall receive a hundredfold, and possess eternal life. But many who are first shall be last, and the last shall be first. This passage is consistent with that statement in which the Savior speaks: I have not come to bring peace, but a sword. For I have come to set a man against his father, and a daughter against her mother, and a daughter-in-law against her mother-in-law; and a man's enemies will be those of his own household (Matthew 10:34). Therefore, because of the faith of Christ and the preaching of the Gospel, those who have disregarded all affections and the riches and pleasures of the world: these will receive a hundredfold and will possess eternal life. From the occasion of this saying, some introduce a thousand years after the resurrection, saying that then a hundredfold of all the things we have given up will be returned to us, and eternal life will be given: not understanding that if in other matters a worthy promise is made, in the matter of wives an indecency appears; so that he who has forsaken one for the Lord may receive a hundred in the future. Therefore, this is the meaning: Whoever forsakes carnal things for the sake of the Savior will receive spiritual things, which will be appropriate and deserved by comparison, as if a hundred is compared to a small number. Hence, the Apostle says, he who has forsaken only one house and small fields of one province: Having nothing, yet possessing everything (II Cor. VI, 10).

<h2 id='tocuniq412'>(Chap. XX.—Vers. 1 seqq.)</h2>

(Chapter 20 — Verses 1 onwards) The kingdom of heaven is like a master of a household who went out early in the morning to hire laborers for his vineyard. After agreeing with the laborers for a denarius a day, he sent them into his vineyard. And going out about the third hour, he saw others standing idle in the marketplace, and he said to them, 'You go into the vineyard too, and whatever is right I will give you.' So they went. Again he went out about the sixth and the ninth hour and did the same. But around the eleventh hour, he went out and found others standing, and he said to them, 'Why are you standing here idle all day?' They said to him, 'Because no one has hired us.' He said to them, 'You also go into the vineyard.' And when evening came, the owner of the vineyard said to his foreman, 'Call the laborers and pay them their wages, beginning with the last up to the first.' So when those who had come at about the eleventh hour came, they each received a denarius. But when the first came, they thought that they would receive more; and they also received each a denarius. And when they had received it, they grumbled against the householder, saying: These last have worked only one hour, and you have made them equal to us, who have borne the burden of the day and the scorching heat? But he replied to one of them, 'Friend, I am doing you no wrong; did you not agree with me for a denarius? Take what belongs to you and go; I choose to give to this last as I give to you.' Are you envious because I am generous?' So the last will be first, and the first last.' For it is written before her: Many will be the first last, and the last first. Not deferring to time, but to faith. And he said to the head of the household to go out early in the morning, to hire workers for his vineyard, and to establish the wage for the work as a denarius. Then, going out around the third hour, he saw others standing idle in the marketplace: and to them, he promised what is just, not a denarius. He also did the same at the sixth and ninth hour. But at the eleventh hour, he found others standing idle all day, and he sent them into his vineyard. And when evening came, he instructed his steward to begin paying the last workers first, from the eleventh hour workers to the first hour workers; and all equally, against those who were hired last, he accused the master of unfairness. Not because they received less than what was agreed upon, but because they wanted to receive more than what the mercy of the employer had poured out upon them. It seems to me that the first hour belongs to the workers Samuel, Jeremiah, and John the Baptist, who can say with the Psalmist: From the womb of my mother you are my God (Psalm 22:10). The third hour belongs to those who have begun to serve God from puberty. The sixth hour belongs to those who have taken on the yoke of Christ in mature age. The ninth hour belongs to those who are already declining towards old age. And the eleventh hour belongs to those in their final old age. Yet all of them receive the same reward, although their labor is different. There are those who explain this parable differently. The first hour represents the time from Adam and the other patriarchs until Noah; the third hour represents the time from Noah until Abraham and the giving of circumcision to him; the sixth hour represents the time from Abraham until Moses when the Law was given; the ninth hour represents Moses and the prophets; the eleventh hour represents the apostles and the Gentile people, who are envied by all. Therefore, understanding this, after the eleventh hour has already passed, when it was near sunset and evening, John the Evangelist says: My little children, it is the last hour (1 John 2:13). And at the same time consider, that the injustice of the father of the family, which all equally accuse in the eleventh hour workers, they do not understand in themselves. For if the father of the family is unjust, he is not unjust in one, but in all: because the worker of the third hour did not work as much as the one who was sent to the vineyard from the first hour. Similarly, the worker of the sixth hour worked less than the worker of the third hour; and the worker of the ninth hour, less than the worker of the sixth hour. Therefore, every backward calling envies the nations, and is twisted in the grace of the Gospel. Hence, the Savior concludes the parable: The first will be last, and the last will be first. That is, the Jews will be turned from head to tail, and we will be changed from tail to head.

<h2 id='tocuniq413'>(Vers. 13.)</h2>

(Verse 13.) Friend, I am not harming you. I read in a certain book that this friend who is reprimanded by the head of the household is understood as the first hour worker, the first created being, and those who believed at that time.

Did you not come with me from the denarius? The denarius has the image of the king. So you received the reward which I promised you, that is, my image and likeness. What more do you seek? And it is not so much that you yourself desire to receive more, but rather that you desire that no one else receive anything, as if the merit of the reward is diminished by the companionship of another.

<h2 id='tocuniq414'>(Vers. 14, 15.)</h2>

(Ver. 14, 15.) Take what is yours and go. But I want to give to this last one as well as to you: Or am I not allowed to do what I want? A Jew is not saved by grace, but by works of the law. For whoever does them, will live by them. Hence it is said to him:

<h2 id='tocuniq415'>(Vers. 16.)</h2>

(Verse 16.) Is your eye evil because I am good? So the last shall be first, and the first last: for many are called, but few chosen. This also sounds the parable of Luke, where the elder son envies the younger, and does not want him to be received back when he repents, and accuses the father of injustice. And so that we know that this is the meaning we have said, the title of this parable and its ending agree. So the last shall be first, and the first last. For many are called, but few are chosen.

<h2 id='tocuniq416'>(Vers. 17 seqq.)</h2>

(Verse 17 and following) And Jesus, going up to Jerusalem, took aside his twelve disciples privately, and said to them: Behold, we are going up to Jerusalem, and the Son of Man will be handed over to the chief priests and scribes, and they will condemn him to death, and they will hand him over to the Gentiles to mock, and to scourge, and to crucify, and on the third day he will rise again. He had often told this to his disciples, but because there were many disputes among them, they could easily forget what they had heard. He was going to Jerusalem, and he was going to bring the apostles with him, preparing them for the temptation: so that when persecution and the ignominy of the cross come, they will not be scandalized.


<h2 id='tocuniq417'>(Vers. 20, 21.)</h2>

(Verse 20, 21.) Then the mother of the sons of Zebedee approached him with her sons, worshiping and asking something from him. He said to her: What do you want? She said to him: Declare that these two sons of mine may sit, one at your right hand and one at your left, in your kingdom. From where does the mother of the sons of Zebedee have the expectation of a kingdom, so that when the Lord says: The son of man will be delivered to the chief priests and scribes, and they will condemn him to death, and they will deliver him to the Gentiles to be mocked, and scourged, and crucified; and when he announces the disgrace of the passion to the disciples who fear, she asks for the glory of triumphing? This, I believe, was the reason why, after saying all these things, the Lord thought that the woman believed that he would immediately reign after the resurrection; and she desired the fulfillment of what is promised in the second coming to happen in the first, and in her feminine eagerness, she desired his presence, forgetting about the future. But when the Lord asked and she requested, it was not out of ignorance; rather, it was said from his perspective, as the one who was to be scourged and crucified, just like with the woman with the issue of blood: 'Who touched me?' (Luke 8:45). And concerning Lazarus: Where have you placed him (John 11:34)? In the Old Testament as well: Adam, where are you (Genesis 3:9)? And: I will go down and see whether they have done according to the cry that has come up to Me; and if not, I will know (Genesis 18:21). But the mother of the sons of Zebedee asks with a mistaken feminine eagerness and a desire for piety, not knowing what she was asking for. And it is not surprising if this is chided as ignorance; for when it is said about Peter, how he wanted to make three tabernacles, not knowing what he was saying (Mark 9).


<h2 id='tocuniq418'>(Vers. 22.)</h2>

(Verse 22.) But Jesus, answering, said: You do not know what you are asking. The mother requests, and the Lord speaks to the disciples, understanding her prayers to come from the will of her sons.

Can you drink the cup that I am about to drink? They said to him: We can. In the divine Scriptures, we understand the cup to signify suffering, according to the saying: Father, if it is possible, let this cup pass from me (Matt. XXVI, 39). And in the psalm: What shall I render to the Lord for all the things that He hath rendered to me? I will take the cup of salvation, and call upon the name of the Lord (Ps. CXV, 3 et seqq.); and immediately it is explained what this cup is: Precious in the sight of the Lord is the death of His saints.


<h2 id='tocuniq419'>(Vers. 23.)</h2>

(Verse 23) He said to them: 'You will indeed drink my chalice; but to sit on my right or left hand is not mine to give to you, but to those for whom it is prepared by my Father.' It is asked how James and John, the sons of Zebedee, drank the chalice of martyrdom, when Scripture records that only James, an Apostle, was beheaded by Herod (Acts 12), while John ended his life by his own death. But if we read the ecclesiastical histories, in which it is reported that he himself was sent for martyrdom into a vat of boiling oil, and from there went forth as an athlete to receive the crown of Christ, and was immediately banished to the island of Patmos, we will see that his spirit was not lacking in martyrdom, and that John drank the chalice of confession, which the three young men also drank in the fiery furnace (Dan. 3), although the persecutor did not shed their blood. But what he said, 'Sit at my right hand and left hand,' is not for me to give, but it is for those for whom it has been prepared by my Father. It is to be understood in this way: the kingdom of heaven is not for someone who gives, but for someone who receives. For there is no favoritism with God (Acts 10:34). But whoever presents themselves in such a way as to become worthy of the kingdom of heaven, they will receive what is prepared not for their person, but for their life. Therefore, if you are such people who will inherit the kingdom of heaven, which my Father has prepared for the triumphant and victorious, you will also receive it. Others want to say about Moses and Elijah: they were speaking with him on the mountain a little while before, but it does not seem to me in any way. For this reason, the words of those seated in the kingdom of heaven are not mentioned, so that if only a few were named, the others would be considered excluded.

<h2 id='tocuniq420'>(Vers. 24.)</h2>

(Verse 24.) And when the ten heard this, they were indignant at the two brothers. The ten apostles were not indignant at the mother of the sons of Zebedee, nor did they attribute her audacity to the brothers; but they attributed it to the fact that, being unaware of their own limits, they were inflamed by excessive desire, to whom the Lord also said: You do not know what you are asking for. It is understood, however, either from the response of the Lord or from the indignation of the apostles, that the sons sent their mother to make great demands.


<h2 id='tocuniq421'>(Vers. 25 seqq.)</h2>

(Verses 25 and following) But Jesus called them to himself and said: You know that the rulers of the Gentiles lord it over them, and those who are great exercise authority over them. It shall not be so among you; but whoever desires to become great among you, let him be your servant. And whoever desires to be first among you, let him be your slave. The humble teacher and gentle, he does not reproach those who seek excessive greed; nor does he rebuke the remaining ten with anger and envy, but he presents such an example to teach that the one who is the least will become the greatest, and the one who serves all will become their master. Therefore, it is in vain that either those people have sought excessive things, or these people grieve over the longing for their ancestors, since the pinnacle of virtues is reached not through power, but through humility. Finally, he presents his own example, so that if his words were considered insignificant, they would be ashamed of their actions, and he says:

<h2 id='tocuniq422'>(Vers. 28.)</h2>

(Verse 28.) Just as the Son of Man did not come to be served, but to serve. Note that we have frequently said that he who serves is called the Son of Man.

And to give his life as redemption for many. When he assumed the form of a servant, in order to shed his blood for the world (Philippians II). He did not say to give his life as redemption for all, but for many, that is, for those who would believe.

<h2 id='tocuniq423'>(Vers. 19 seqq.)</h2>

(Verse 19 and following) And as they were leaving Jericho, a great crowd followed him. And behold, two blind men sitting by the roadside, heard that Jesus was passing by, and they cried out, saying: Lord, have mercy on us, Son of David. But the crowd rebuked them, telling them to be silent. But they cried out all the more, saying: Lord, have mercy on us, Son of David. There were many robbers in Jericho, who used to attack and harm those who were coming in and going out of Jerusalem: therefore, the Lord came to Jericho with his disciples, to heal the wounded and to gather a large crowd with him." } Finally, after they wanted to leave from Jericho, a large crowd followed him. If he had stayed in Jerusalem and never descended to lowly places, the crowd would still be sitting in darkness and in the shadow of death to this day. However, there were also two blind men beside the road. He calls the blind men those who were not yet able to speak: In your light we will see light (Ps. XXXV, 10). Along the road, they seemed to have knowledge of the Law, but they were ignorant of the way which is Christ: whom most people understand as the Pharisees and Sadducees, and others as both the people of the old Testament and the new: one of them, following the written Law, was blind without Christ, while the other followed natural law. Because they were unable to see by themselves, they heard the proclamations of the Savior, and confessed him as the son of David. But if both blind men are referred to the Jewish people, what follows, "The crowd rebuked them," is to be understood in reference to the Gentiles, whom the Apostle warns not to boast and be arrogant against their own root (Rom. XI); but since they themselves were grafted into the good olive tree from the wild olive tree through the error of the previous people, they should by no means envy the salvation of the previous people. Have mercy on us, O son of David. They are being rebuked by the crowds, and yet they do not remain silent: but rather they repeat the same thing more frequently, so that they may reveal the full desire for true light.

<h2 id='tocuniq424'>(Vers. 32, 33.)</h2>

(Verse 32, 33.) And Jesus stood still, and called them, and said, 'What do you want me to do for you?' They said to him, 'Lord, let our eyes be opened.' They were blind, they did not know where to go, and they could not follow the Savior. There were many pits in Jericho, many rocks and cliffs plunging into the depths: therefore the Lord stands, so that they can come and be called, lest the crowds hinder them: and he asks as if not knowing what they want, so that from the response of the blind, their weakness may be made clear, and their power may be known from the remedy.


<h2 id='tocuniq425'>(Vers. 34.)</h2>

(Verse 34) But Jesus, having compassion on them, touched their eyes; and immediately they saw, and followed him. He touches their eyes and the artist gives what nature did not give. Or certainly what weakness had taken away, mercy gives. And immediately they saw, and followed him. Those who were sitting before in Jericho, blind and begging, knew only how to cry out. But afterwards they followed Jesus, not so much with their feet as with their virtues.

<h2 id='tocuniq426'>(Chap. XXI.—Vers. 1 seqq.)</h2>

(Chapter 21 - Verses 1 onward) And when he drew near to Jerusalem and came to Bethphage, to the Mount of Olives, then Jesus sent two disciples, saying to them: 'Go into the village opposite you, and immediately you will find a donkey tied, and a colt with her. Untie them and bring them to me. And if anyone says anything to you, you shall say, The Lord needs them, and he will send them at once.' He went out from Jericho, with a multitude following him, and receiving sight, he drew near to Jerusalem, greatly enriched with merchandise. The health of believers being restored, he desires to enter the city of peace, and the place of God's vision, and the stronghold of the watchtower. And when he was approaching to Jerusalem, and had come to Bethphage, to the house of the jaws (which was a village of the priests, and bore the semblance of confession; and it was situated on the mount of Olives, where the light of knowledge, where the rest of labors and sorrows), he sent two of his disciples, the contemplative and the active, that is, knowledge and labor, to enter the fortress. And he said to them: Go into the fortress that is opposite you. For it was against the apostles, and he did not want to accept the yoke of teachings. And immediately, he said, you will find a tied donkey and its foal with her: untie them and bring them to me. The donkey was bound by many sins. The foal, too, was unruly and impatient with the reins, according to the Gospel of Luke (Chapter 19), it had many owners, not subject to one error and doctrine: and yet many owners who claimed illicit power for themselves, seeing the true Lord and his servants who had come to set them free, did not dare to resist. We will say what a female donkey, and a foal of a donkey, are in the following lines.

<h2 id='tocuniq427'>(Vers. 4, 5.)</h2>

(Vers. 4, 5.) However, this happened so that what was said by the prophet might be fulfilled, saying: "Tell the daughter of Zion, Look, your king comes to you, meek, sitting on a donkey, and on a colt, the foal of a pack animal." This is written in the prophet Zechariah: of whom, if there is enough time in life, it will be spoken in its proper place. Now, briefly to be noted, that according to the literal sense, he was not able to sit on both animals in the small distance of the journey. For either the donkey sat, and the foal was without a rider; or if the foal, which is more fitting, it was used for sitting, the donkey was led free. Therefore, when the story has either impossibility or disgrace, we are led to deeper things: so that that donkey which was submissive, and tamed, and had taken on the yoke of the law, may be understood as the Synagogue; the foal of the donkey, wanton and free, may be understood as the people of the Gentiles, whom Jesus sat upon, and sending his two disciples to them, one to the Circumcision, and the other to the Gentiles.


<h2 id='tocuniq428'>(Vers. 6, 7.)</h2>

(Verse 6, 7) However, the disciples went and did as Jesus had directed them. They brought the donkey and the colt, and put their clothes on them, and he sat on them. These colt and donkey, on which the apostles spread their clothes for Jesus to sit more comfortably, were naked before the coming of the Savior, and they suffered from those who claimed dominion over them without any covering. But after they received the apostolic garments, they became more beautiful and had the Lord as their rider. Apostolic clothing can be understood as either the teaching of virtues or the exposition of Scriptures, or the various doctrines of the Church: unless the soul is instructed and adorned by these, it does not deserve to have the Lord as its guest.

<h2 id='tocuniq429'>(Vers. 8.)</h2>

(Verse 8) However, many people spread their clothes on the road. Notice the difference in each person. The apostles put their clothes on the donkey, while the crowd that is less important, lays them on the donkey's feet; so that it does not stumble on a stone, step on a thorn, or fall into a pit.

But others were cutting branches from the trees and spreading them on the road. From the fruit-bearing trees, with which the Mount of Olives is planted, they were cutting branches and spreading them on the road in order to make the rough ways smooth and the uneven paths level, so that Christ, the conqueror of demons and vices, could walk more rightly and securely in the hearts of believers.

<h2 id='tocuniq430'>(Vers. 9.)</h2>

(Verse 9.) But the crowds that went ahead of him and those that followed were shouting, 'Hosanna to the Son of David!' 'Blessed is he who comes in the name of the Lord!' 'Hosanna in the highest heaven!' For the story is clear, let us follow the spiritual order of discourse. The crowds that had come out of Jericho and had followed the Savior and his disciples, when they saw the colt of a donkey untied (which had been tied up before) and adorned with the apostles' robes, and the Lord Jesus sitting on it, they put their garments on the colt and spread branches of trees on the road. And when they had done all things, they also give testimony of the voice: and preceding and following not with a brief and silent confession, but with the fullest shout: Hosanna to the Son of David, blessed is He Who comes in the name of the Lord. But what He says: The crowds that went before Him and that followed cried out, shows both the Jewish people who believed before the Gospel and those who believed after the Gospel praising Jesus with the same confession of faith: and according to the example of the parable of the workers in the vineyard, receiving the same reward for their faith. Furthermore, regarding what follows: "Hosanna to the Son of David," I remember having said many years ago in a short letter to Damasus, the bishop of the city of Rome at that time; and now I will explain briefly. In the 117th Psalm, which is clearly written about the coming of the Savior, among other things we also read this: 'The stone that the builders rejected has become the cornerstone.' This is the Lord's doing; it is marvelous in our eyes. This is the day which the Lord has made, let us rejoice and be glad in it. Immediately follows: O Lord, save me; O Lord, grant success. Blessed is he who comes in the name of the Lord; we bless you from the house of the Lord, etc. (Psalm 118:22 ff). Because it is found in the Septuagint interpreters; ὦ Κύριε, σῶσον δὴ, that is, O Lord, save; in Hebrew we read: Anna Adonai Osianna (), which is more clearly interpreted by Symmachus saying: I beseech you, Lord, save, I beseech. Therefore, no one should think that the phrase is composed of two words, namely Greek and Hebrew, but it is entirely Hebrew and signifies that the coming of Christ is the salvation of the world. Hence it follows: Blessed is he who comes in the name of the Lord. This is also confirmed by the Savior himself in the Gospel: I came in my Father's name, and you did not receive me. Another will come in his own name, and you will receive him (John 5:43). Likewise, it is clearly shown by the joining of 'Hosanna,' that is, salvation in the highest, that the coming of Christ is not only the salvation of humanity, but of the whole world, joining the earthly with the heavenly: that every knee should bow, of those in heaven, on earth, and under the earth (Philippians 2:10).


<h2 id='tocuniq431'>(Vers. 10.)</h2>

(Version 10.) And when he entered Jerusalem, the whole city was stirred up, saying, Who is this? And the people said, When Jesus entered with the whole crowd, the city of Jerusalem was stirred up, marveling at the large number of people, not knowing the truth, and saying, Who is this? Which indeed we read elsewhere the angels saying, Who is this king of glory? (Psalm 24:8) But to others, who were either confused or questioning, the common people confess, starting from the least, in order to arrive at the greater, and they say:

163 This is Jesus, the prophet from Nazareth in Galilee. The prophet whom Moses had said would come, who is also called The Messiah in Greek. From Nazareth in Galilee, because he was raised there: like a flower of the field, nurtured in the bloom of virtues.

<h2 id='tocuniq432'>(Vers. 12, 13.)</h2>

(Vers. 12, 13.) And Jesus entered into the temple of God and drove out all those who were selling and buying in the temple, and overturned the tables of the money changers and the seats of those selling doves. And He said to them: It is written, My house shall be called a house of prayer, but you have made it a den of thieves. Jesus, accompanied by a crowd of believers who had spread their garments so that a young colt could walk on them without being harmed, entered the temple and drove out all those who were selling and buying in the temple. He overturned the tables of the money changers and scattered the seats of those selling doves. And He said to them, quoting from the Holy Scriptures (Isaiah 56) and (Jeremiah 7), that the house of His Father should be a house of prayer, not a den of thieves or a house of commerce, as it is written in another Gospel (John 2). From this it must first be known, that according to the commands of the most august Law, in the whole temple of the Lord, and from almost all the regions of Judea, people were flocking there, countless victims were sacrificed, chiefly on feast days, of bulls, rams, goats; in order that the poor might not be without offerings, those who offered young pigeons and turtledoves: it frequently happened, therefore, that those who had come from afar did not have victims. Accordingly, the priests devised a way to make profit from the people, and they sold all the animals which were needed for the sacrifices, so that they might both sell to those who did not have them, and buy again for themselves. He dissipated this obstacle by the frequent funding of those who were in need, and not only did they not have 164 victims, but they did not even have birds and cheap gifts to buy them. Therefore, they also appointed moneylenders who would lend money under guarantee. But because it was commanded by the Law (Leviticus 25 and Deuteronomy 23) that no interest should be taken, and borrowed money could not be profitable, as it had no advantage and sometimes lost its fortune, they devised another technique: to make collybistas instead of moneylenders, whose term is not expressed in the Latin language. They are called collybae, what we call tragemata, or cheap gifts. For example, roasted chickpeas, dried grapes, and various types of fruits. Therefore, because the collybistae, who were moneylenders, could not collect interest, they received various goods as interest, so that they could demand what was not allowed in money, in those things that are purchased with money. As if Ezekiel himself did not preach this, saying: You shall not take interest and increase (Ezekiel 22:12). Seeing this kind of Lord observing the business or robbery in the Father's house, spurred on by the zeal of the Spirit (according to what is written in Psalm 68:10: The zeal of your house consumes me), he made for himself a whip out of cords, and drove out such a great crowd of people from the temple, saying: It is written: My house shall be called a house of prayer; but you have made it a den of thieves. For he is a thief, and he turns the temple of God into a den of thieves, pursuing profits from religion, and his worship is not so much the worship of God as an opportunity for business. According to history, however, according to mystical understanding, Jesus enters the temple of the Father every day and casts out all, both bishops, and priests, and deacons, as well as laypeople, and the whole crowd from His Church, and He accuses them of the same sin as both sellers and buyers. For it is written: You received without payment; give without payment. He also overturns the tables of the money changers. Observe, because of the greed of the priests, the altars of God are called the tables of money changers. And he overturned the tables of those who were selling doves, who were selling the grace of the Holy Spirit, and they do everything to devour the subject people, of whom it says (As it is said): Those who devour my people like bread (Psalm 14:4). According to simple understanding, doves were not in chairs but in cages, unless perhaps the sellers of the doves were sitting in chairs. But this is completely absurd, because in chairs, the dignity of teachers is indicated, which is reduced to nothing when it is mixed with profits. As for what we have said about the churches, let each one understand it regarding themselves. For the Apostle says: 'You are the temple of God, and the Holy Spirit dwells in you' (1 Corinthians 6:19, 2 Corinthians 3:16). Let there be no dealing in the house of our heart, no buying and selling, no desire for material possessions, so that Jesus may not enter angry and stern, and so that He may not cleanse His temple in any other way than by using a whip, just as He made a house of prayer out of the den of thieves and the house of trade." }


<h2 id='tocuniq433'>(Vers. 14.)</h2>

(Verse 14.) And the blind and the lame came to him in the temple, and he healed them. Unless he had overturned the tables of the money changers and the chairs of those selling doves, the blind and the lame would not have received their former sight and their restored ability to walk.

<h2 id='tocuniq434'>(Vers. 15.)</h2>

(Verse 15) But when the chief priests and scribes saw the wonders He had performed, and the children crying out in the temple, saying, 'Hosanna to the Son of David,' they were indignant and said to Him, 'Do You hear what these children are saying?' Many people believe that the raising of Lazarus (John 11), the blind man receiving sight from birth (John 9), the voice heard at the Jordan saying, 'This is my beloved Son' (John 3), and the transfiguration on the mountain revealing His triumphant glory (Matthew 17) are the greatest signs. Among all the signs that Jesus performed, what seems to be more amazing to me is that one man, and at that time despised and so lowly that he would later be crucified, was able to drive out such a large crowd with the scourge of one whip, overturn tables, break chairs, and do other things that not even an infinite number of armies could have done. For there was something fiery and celestial radiating from his eyes, and the majesty of divinity was shining on his face. And although the priests did not dare to lay hands on him, they still accused him of deceit, and they turned the testimony of the people and children who were shouting, 'Hosanna to the Son of David,' into an accusation; for they knew that this could only be said of the Son of God alone. Therefore, let the bishops and any holy men see with what danger they allow these things to be said of themselves, if the one to whom it was truly said, because the faith of the believers was not yet solid, is being blamed for a crime.


<h2 id='tocuniq435'>(Vers. 16.)</h2>

(Verse 16) But Jesus said to them, 'Surely you have never read, "Out of the mouths of infants and nursing babies you have prepared praise for yourself?"' How moderately he tempers his statement, and his response inclines both ways, and he does not tolerate slander. He did not say what the Scribes desired to hear, that the children are doing good and giving witness to me, nor again, they are wrong: they are children, you should forgive their age. But he presents an example from the eighth psalm, in order to confirm the testimony of the Scriptures through the words of children in the silence of the Lord.


<h2 id='tocuniq436'>(Vers. 17.)</h2>

(Verse 17) And leaving them, he went out outside the city to Bethany, and stayed there. He left the unbelievers and, leaving the city of contradictors, he went to Bethany, which means the house of obedience, foreshadowing the calling of the gentiles, and stayed there, because he could not remain in Israel. This is also to be understood, that he was of such great poverty, and was not flattered by anyone, so that in the great city he found no guest, no dwelling, but stayed in a small field at Lazarus' and his sisters' house: for their village is Bethany.


<h2 id='tocuniq437'>(Vers. 18, seqq.)</h2>

(Verse 18 and following) But in the morning, as he returned to the city, he was hungry. And seeing a fig tree by the side of the road, he came to it and found nothing on it but leaves only. And he said to it, 'May no fruit ever come from you again!' And immediately the fig tree withered away. And when the disciples saw it, they marveled, saying, 'How did the fig tree wither at once?' Dispersing the darkness of the night, with the morning light shining and nearing midday, in which the Lord was to enlighten the world through his passion, when he returned to the city, he was hungry, either showing the truth of his human flesh or hungering for the salvation of believers and burning with indignation toward the unbelief of the people of Israel. And when he saw one tree (which we understand to be the Synagogue and assembly of the Jews) by the road: for it had the Law, and therefore it was by the road, because it did not believe in the way, he came to it, standing namely and immobile, and not having the feet of the Gospel: and he found nothing in it, except leaves only, the noise of promises, Pharisaic traditions, and the boasting of the Law, and the ornaments of words without any fruits of truth. And another evangelist says, For it was not yet time (Mark 10:13); either because the time of Israel's salvation had not yet come, since the population of the Gentiles had not yet entered, or because the time of faith had passed, because coming to Him first and being rejected, it passed on to the nations. And He said to him, May fruit never be born from you, either in eternity or in this age; for the Greek word αἰὼν (Al. αἰῶνα) signifies both. And the basket was emptied, which had the food that the hungry Lord desired. But just as the leaves withered, so that only the trunk remained, and with the branches broken, the root flourished. If it wishes to believe in the last times, may the faith sprout like branches, and may the Scripture be fulfilled, saying: 'There is hope for a tree.' But according to the literal sense, the Lord, about to suffer among the people and to carry the scandal of the cross, ought to strengthen the minds of the disciples with the anticipation of the sign. And the disciples also marveled, saying: How did it immediately wither? 168 Therefore, by the power of his Savior, he could also dry up his enemies, unless he had awaited their salvation through repentance.

<h2 id='tocuniq438'>(Vers. 21, 22.)</h2>

(Verse 21, 22.) But Jesus, answering, said to them: Amen I say to you, if you have faith and do not hesitate, not only will you do what has been done to the fig tree, but even if you say to this mountain, 'Take up and throw yourself into the sea,' it will be done. And whatever you ask for in prayer, believing, you will receive. Gentile dogs bark against us in their books, which they have left as a memorial of their own impiety, asserting that the apostles did not have faith because they were unable to move mountains. To whom shall we respond that many signs have been done by the Lord, according to the testimony of John the Evangelist, which could not be written down because the world could not contain them (John 21:25). Not that the world could not contain the volumes, which it can, even though they are numerous, in one small box or in one bookshelf, but because the magnitude of the signs could not be borne by miracles and disbelief. Therefore, we believe that the apostles also did these things, but they were not written down so that unbelievers would not have more occasion to contradict. Furthermore, let us ask them whether they believe in these signs which are told in the scriptures or not. And when we see them to be unbelievers, we will consequently prove that those who did not believe in the lesser signs will not believe in the greater ones. This is against them. However, as we have already said, let us understand the proud devil as a mountain, and boasting against his Creator, who is called by the prophet a corrupted mountain. And when he possesses the soul of a man, and is rooted in it, he can be transferred by the apostles and those who are similar to the apostles into the sea, that is, into salty, rough, and bitter places that have no sweetness of God. And in the Psalms it is read: We shall not fear when the earth is troubled, and the mountains are carried into the heart of the sea (Ps. 46:2).

<h2 id='tocuniq439'>(Vers. 23.)</h2>

(Verse 23.) And when He had come into the temple, the chief priests and the elders of the people came to Him as He was teaching, and said, 'By what authority are You doing these things? And who gave You this authority?' In different words, they construct the same accusation as before, when they said, 'He casts out demons by the ruler of the demons, Beelzebub.' For when they say, 'By what authority are You doing these things?' they doubt the authority of God, and they want it to be understood that it is the devil who is doing these things. Moreover, they ask, 'Who gave you this authority?' They clearly deny the Son of God, whom they think is not able to perform miracles by his own power, but by the power of others.

<h2 id='tocuniq440'>(Vers. 24 seqq.)</h2>

(Verse 24 and following) But Jesus answered and said to them: I also will ask you one question, which if you tell me, I likewise will tell you by what authority I do these things. The baptism of John, where was it from: from heaven or from men? And they thought within themselves, saying: If we say, From heaven; he will say to us: Why then did you not believe him? But if we say, From men, we fear the people. For all held John as a prophet. And answering, they said: We do not know. This is what is commonly said: A bad knot of a tree, a bad nail or wedge must be driven in. The Lord could refute the calumny of the tempters with an open response, but He wisely asks, so that they themselves, either by their silence or by their opinion, may be condemned. For if they had answered that the baptism of John is from heaven (as they, wise in evil, have considered), the consequent answer would be: Why then were you not baptized by John? If they wanted to say that it was a human invention and had nothing divine, they feared the revolt of the people. For all the crowds had received John's baptism in groups, and they regarded him as a prophet. Therefore, the most wicked faction responded and used the word of humility, by which they claimed not to know, to lay traps.

<h2 id='tocuniq441'>(Vers. 27.)</h2>

(Verse 27.) He also said to them: Neither do I tell you by what authority I do these things. They answered Him, therefore: We do not know. He replied then, saying likewise: Neither do I tell you. But Truth cannot lie, and He said: Neither do I tell you. From this, He showed that they knew but did not want to answer, and that He knew and therefore did not speak, because they were silent about what they knew. And immediately He gave a parable, which both condemned their impiousness and taught that the kingdom of God should be transferred to the nations.


<h2 id='tocuniq442'>(Vers. 28 seqq.)</h2>

(Verse 28 onwards) 170 What do you think? A certain man had two sons; and he came to the first, and said: Son, go work today in my vineyard. And he answering, said: I will not. But afterward, being moved with repentance, he went. And coming to the other, he said in like manner. And he answering, said: I go, sir; and he went not. Which of the two did the will of the father? They say to him: The first. Jesus said to them (Luke 15). There are two sons, who are described in the parable of Luke, the frugal and the prodigal, and of whom the prophet Zacharias speaks: I took two rods for myself, one I called Beauty, and the other I called Cord, and I fed the flock (Zach. XI, 7). First, it is said to the Gentile people through the knowledge of natural law: Go and work in my vineyard: that is, what you do not want done to you, do not do to others (Tob. IV). He proudly replied: I do not want to. Afterwards, in the arrival of the Savior, there was an act of repentance and the working in the vineyard of God, and he corrected the obstinacy of his speech through labor. However, the second son, the people of the Jews, responded to Moses, 'We will do everything that the Lord has said' (Exod. XXIV), but he did not go into the vineyard because he thought he would be the heir after killing the son of the household. Others, however, do not consider it a parable of the Gentiles and the Jews, but simply of sinners and the righteous, with the Lord himself explaining his parable later.


Truly I say to you, the tax collectors and the prostitutes go into the kingdom of God before you. For John came to you in the way of righteousness, and you did not believe him; but the tax collectors and the prostitutes believed him; and even when you saw it, you did not afterward repent and believe him.

<h2 id='tocuniq443'>(Vers. 32.)</h2>

(Verse 32) Indeed, John came to you in the way of righteousness, and you did not believe him; but tax collectors and prostitutes believed him; and even when you saw it, you did not afterward change your minds and believe him. Therefore, which of the two sons did the will of his father? And they said, The last. It is to be noted that in the true manuscripts, the word 'last' is not present, but rather 'first,' so that they may be condemned by their own judgment. However, if we wish to read 'last,' the interpretation is clear: that we may say the Jews understood the truth, but twisted it and refused to say what they thought, just as they also refused to acknowledge that John's baptism was from heaven.


<h2 id='tocuniq444'>(Vers. 33.)</h2>

(Verse 33) Listen to another parable: A man was a master of a household, who planted a vineyard and put a fence around it and dug a winepress in it and built a tower, and he leased it to farmers and went on a journey. This is what the Lord illustrated from a proverb: It is hard to kick against the pricks (Acts 9:5). The chief priests and the elders of the people, who questioned the Lord, asked Him, 'By what authority are you doing these things? And who gave you this authority?' And they desired to grasp wisdom in word, they are overcome by their own craft; and they hear in parables, what they did not deserve to hear with open face. This man, the father of a family, is the one who had two sons; and in another parable, he hired workers in his vineyard; he planted a vineyard, of which Isaiah speaks fully through the Song, bringing it to the end: The vineyard of the Lord of hosts is the house of Israel (Isaiah 5:7). And in the Psalm: You brought a vine out of Egypt, it says, you cast out the nations, and planted it (Psalm 80:9). And he surrounded her with a hedge, either with walls or with the help of angels: and he dug a winepress, or an altar, or those winepresses, of which three Psalms are designated by title: the eighty-eighth, and the eighty-third. And he built a tower: undoubtedly a temple, of which it is said through Michaeas: And thou, tower of mist, daughter of Zion (Mic. IV, 8). And he placed it among the farmers, whom elsewhere he called the workers of the vineyard: who were hired at the first, third, sixth, ninth, and eleventh hour (Matt. X). And he journeyed abroad: abroad, not by a change of place; for where can God be absent, when all things are filled with Him? And he who says through Jeremiah: I am a God who is near and not far away, says the Lord (Jeremiah 23:23). But he seems to depart from the vineyard, so as to leave the vinedressers free will to work.

<h2 id='tocuniq445'>(Vers. 34 seqq.)</h2>

(v. 34 seqq.) But when the time of the fruits approached, he sent his servants to the farmers to receive his fruits. And the farmers, seizing his servants, some they beat, some they killed, and some they stoned. Again he sent other servants, more than the first, and they did the same to them. He had given them the Law, and in this he had ordered them to work in his vineyard, so that they would produce the fruit of the Law in their deeds. Afterwards he sent to them servants whom they had seized or killed, like Jeremiah, or killed, like Isaiah, or stoned, like Naboth (3 Kings 21), and Zachariah, whom they killed between the temple and the altar (2 Chronicles 24)." } Let us read the letter of Paul to the Hebrews (Heb. XI); and from it, we will fully learn how much the servants of the Lord have suffered.

<h2 id='tocuniq446'>(Vers. 37, 38.)</h2>

(Verse 37, 38.) But lastly, he sent his son to them, saying: They will respect my son. But the vine-dressers, seeing the son, said among themselves: This is the heir; come, let us kill him, and we shall have his inheritance. In what we read above: Again he sent other servants more than the first, and they did the same to them, the patience of the master of the household is shown, that he sent them more frequently, in order to provoke the wicked tenants to repentance: but they treasured up anger for themselves on the day of anger (Romans 2). Furthermore, that which is joined: They will fear my son, not out of ignorance. For what does the father of the family not know, who in this place is understood as God the Father? But God is always said to be in doubt, so that free will may be reserved for man. Let us question Arius and Eunomius. Look, the father is said to be ignorant, and he tempers his judgment, and as much as is in you, it is proven that he has lied. Whatever they may answer on behalf of the Father, may they understand it to be on behalf of the Son, who says that he does not know the day of consummation.


<h2 id='tocuniq447'>(Vers. 39.)</h2>

(Verse 39.) And they seized him, cast him out of the vineyard, and killed him. And the Apostle speaks, that Jesus was crucified outside the gate (Heb. XIII). We can also understand it differently; that he was cast out of the vineyard and killed there: so that when the gentiles received him, the vineyard would be given to others.

<h2 id='tocuniq448'>(Vers. 40, 41.)</h2>

(Verse 40, 41.) So when the Lord of the vineyard comes, what will he do to those vine-dressers? They said to him: He will destroy those evil ones, and he will give his vineyard to other vine-dressers who will give him the fruit in its proper season. The Lord questioned them not because he did not know what they would respond, but so that by their own response they would be condemned. A vineyard has been leased to us, and it has been leased to us with the condition that we give the Lord the fruit in its proper season, and that we know at each time what we ought to speak or to do.


<h2 id='tocuniq449'>(Vers. 42.)</h2>

(Verse 42.) Jesus said to them: Have you never read in the Scriptures: 'The stone that the builders rejected has become the cornerstone. This was the Lord's doing and it is marvelous in our eyes' (Psalm 118:22, 23). Various parables and different teachings are woven together in this matter. For those whom Jesus had previously called workers, vineyard keepers, and farmers, he now calls builders, that is, masons. Where the Apostle says: You are God's field, God's building (1 Corinthians 3:9). These masons, therefore, just as vine-dressers receive a vine, so have received a stone, which they should place either in the foundations, according to the architect Paul (ibid.), or in the corner, so that it joins together two walls, that is, both peoples (Ephesians 2), who, rejected by them, have become the head cornerstone. And this was done by the Lord, not by human strength, but by the power of God. Peter also confidently speaks about this stone of help: This stone, which was rejected by you builders, has become the cornerstone (1 Peter 2:7). And Isaiah says: Behold, I will lay in Zion a chosen, precious, cornerstone; and whoever believes in Him will not be put to shame (Isaiah 28:16).

<h2 id='tocuniq450'>(Vers. 43.)</h2>

(Verse 43.) Therefore, I tell you, the kingdom of God will be taken away from you and given to a nation producing its fruits. I have said many times that the kingdom of God is to be understood in the Holy Scriptures, which the Lord took away from the Jews and handed over to us, so that we may produce its fruits. This is the vineyard that is entrusted to farmers and vine-growers, and those who have not worked in it, only having the name of the Scriptures, will lose the fruits of the vineyard.

<h2 id='tocuniq451'>(Vers. 44.)</h2>

(Verse 44.) And whoever falls on this stone will be broken: but on whomsoever it shall fall, it will grind him to powder. It is one thing to offend Christ through evil deeds, and another to deny Him. The sinner, even though he believes in Him, falls indeed upon the stone and is broken, but not completely destroyed; he is saved through repentance. But on whomsoever that stone shall fall, that is, on him who the stone itself falls upon, and who completely denies Christ, it will grind him to powder, so that not even a shard will remain in which a little water may be poured.


<h2 id='tocuniq452'>(Vers. 45, 46.)</h2>

(V. 45, 46.) And when the chief priests and Pharisees heard his parables, they understood that he was talking about them. And wanting to arrest him, they feared the crowds, because they considered him a prophet. Although they had hard hearts and were dull in their belief and wickedness toward the Son of God, they could not deny the clear statements, and they knew that all of the Lord's judgments were directed against them. Indeed, they wanted to kill him, but they were afraid of the crowds because they regarded him as a prophet. The crowd is always fickle, not remaining steadfast in their purpose, and like the sway of waves, they are dragged here and there by the force of different winds. Now they revere and honor him as if he were a prophet, but later they cry out against him, saying, 'Crucify him, crucify such a person.' (John 19:6).

<h2 id='tocuniq453'>(Chap. XXII.—Vers. 1, 2.)</h2>

Chapter XXII - Verse 1, 2. Jesus answered and spoke again in parables to them, saying: The kingdom of heaven is like a king who prepared a wedding feast for his son. The Pharisees, understanding that the parables were about them, sought to hold him and kill him. Knowing their intention, the Lord reproached them, undeterred by fear, in order to convict the sinners. This king who prepared a wedding feast for his son is almighty God. He establishes the marriage between our Lord Jesus Christ and the Church, which is gathered from both Jews and Gentiles.

<h2 id='tocuniq454'>(Vers. 3.)</h2>

(Verse 3.) And he sent his servant to call the invited ones to the wedding, but they did not want to come. Without a doubt, Moses, through whom the Law was given to the invited ones. But if we read the servants, as most examples have, it must be referred to the prophets that the invited ones disdained to come through them.

<h2 id='tocuniq455'>(Vers. 4, 5.)</h2>

(Verse 4, 5.) Again he sent other servants, saying: Tell those who are invited, Behold, I have prepared my dinner: my oxen and fatlings are killed, and all things are ready: come to the wedding. But they paid no attention. The servants who were sent a second time are better understood as prophets than as apostles, though if in the previous verse the word 'servant' is written, the same servants are meant. But if you read 'servants' there, these second servants are to be understood as apostles. The prepared dinner and the killed oxen and fatlings either describe the wealth of the king by metaphor, so that spiritual things are understood from carnal ones, or certainly the greatness of teachings and the doctrine of God's fullest law can be understood.


<h2 id='tocuniq456'>(Vers. 6.)</h2>

(Verse 6) And they went away, one to his own village, and another to his business. But the rest seized his servants and, treating them with contempt, killed them. Among those who do not accept the truth of the Gospel, there is much diversity. Those who refused to come, being occupied with other matters, are guilty of a lesser crime than those who, disregarding the affection of the inviter, turned humanity into cruelty and either mistreated or killed the king's servants. In this parable, the silent killing of the groom is depicted: and through the deaths of the servants, the contempt of the wedding is shown.

<h2 id='tocuniq457'>(Vers. 7.)</h2>

(Verse 7) But when the king heard this, he was angry. This is similar to what was said above: The kingdom of heaven is like a king who invited people to a wedding feast and showed kindness to them. The king is referred to as a man. But now, when it comes to vengeance, the man is silent and only the king is mentioned.

And having sent his armies, he destroyed those murderers and burned their city. The armies, or rather avenging angels, of whom it is written in the Psalms: He sent upon them the wrath of his indignation: indignation, and wrath, and trouble, which he sent by evil angels. (Psalm 77:49); or we may understand the Romans, under the leadership of Vespasian and Titus, who, having killed the people of Judea, burned the rebellious city.

<h2 id='tocuniq458'>(Vers. 8 seqq.)</h2>

Then he said to his servants: The wedding is ready, but those who were invited were not worthy. Therefore go to the exits of the highways, and whoever you find, invite them to the wedding. And his servants went out into the highways and gathered together all whom they found, both bad and good, and the wedding hall was filled with guests.
